# Build Three Real-World Python Applications

> Python skills are in high demand, and programmers who demonstrate real-world skill in building applications are highly sought after. In this course, Megan Silvey shows you how to take real-life programming problems and turn them into actionable results through building three different practical applications. These applications focus on working with popular Python topics: web scraping, web APIs, an

### 1. Scraping the Wisdom Pet Medicine Website

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### What is web scraping?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=0)** - Have you ever wanted to go see a movie, but you really wanted to understand the reviews ahead of time?
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=7)** One way you could do this is by utilizing web scraping.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=11)** So what is web scraping?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=14)** Web scraping is a process used to extract data from a webpage.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=19)** In this case, the data we gather is the HTML code on the webpage, sometimes we want together text data from this HTML code and other times we may want to gather numerical values.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=31)** Web scraping may also be referred to by different names, such as web data extraction, screen scraping, or web harvesting.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=38)** In this course, we will only call it web scraping to keep things simple.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=43)** We use what is called an HTTP request to gather the HTML code from the server.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=49)** Once we run our HTTP request, then we can view, manipulate, and save the HTML code we pulled.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=57)** So why would we want to scrape HTML code in the first place?
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=61)** Web scraping has a variety of uses, such as scraping product reviews, monitoring the weather, gathering real-time stock data, and populating newsfeeds.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=71)** There are also many programming languages that can perform web scraping, such as Python, C++, Java, and Ruby.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=78)** Python is a top choice for many programmers due to its simple syntax and versatile applications when it comes to handling scrape data among performing other tasks.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=89)** With the many websites available on the web, there are a few things to keep in mind when it comes to being a good steward of data.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=97)** First, make sure you are only scraping public websites and following their terms and conditions.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=102)** This is very important if you're scraping websites for commercial use, since you often need permission from the owner of the website to scrape it.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=110)** Also make sure you avoid any unnecessary or excessive web scraping to not only best manage your resources, but to also avoid becoming blocked from the webpage.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=121)** You'll want to properly manage your expectations when web scraping.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=126)** The HTML code behind websites can often change, causing errors to potentially arise in your code that may not have been there before.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=133)** For example, if you were scraping a certain section that no longer exists on a webpage, then your code will most likely give you an error that it cannot find that HTML code anymore.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=144)** Additionally, certain websites will not allow you to scrape their HTML code directly from their webpage.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=149)** Some will not allow any web scraping at all, and others will give you an option to use an API they created to scrape the HTML code instead.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=158)** We will review what APIs are and how they work later in the course.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=162)** Throughout the following videos, we will show the basics of scraping a webpage.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=167)** These web scraping techniques can be used on many of the websites available on the World Wide Web.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-web-scraping?u=76281980&t=172)** We will focus on one website for the duration of this chapter to keep things simple.

#### Understanding HTML code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Let's take a moment to understand the general structure of HTML code.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=4)** You'll notice that HTML code has a lot of similarities in its structure compared to Python.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=9)** For example, there are indents for code to function within other code, functions we can create, and different variables and classes we can use.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=18)** One major difference between the HTML code and Python code is tags.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=22)** HTML code is heavily dependent on tags, where every piece of code needs to have an opening tag and a closing tag.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=30)** These tags are necessary in order for the web browser to understand how the webpage should be structured, where images need to be located, and what should be a clickable link for web users.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=41)** For example, the HTML tag is needed for every webpage since this signals to the web browser that this is an HTML document.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=48)** We can see this if we close the body tag.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=51)** Every webpage has these HTML tags at the very top and very bottom of the HTML code to help containerize the rest of the HTML code within it.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=61)** All right, let's open our body tag.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=64)** And so now we can see that there are many other tags in our HTML code and many more that are not in our code but may be in other webpages.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=72)** For example, there is the body tag, header tags, div tags, and many more.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=78)** You will also notice that some tags have a class or other information within them to further define how they are incorporated in the code.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=85)** This helps make them much easier to search.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=89)** I highly recommend you take some time on your own to review HTML code and its tags.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=94)** You do not need to be an expert in coding in HTML, but a general idea of how HTML code works and its structure will be helpful for better understanding the following videos.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-html-code?u=76281980&t=105)** You can go to the [[HTML Essential Training]] LinkedIn course listed here, or you can explore some other webpages on your own to familiarize yourself with the HTML code behind them.

#### Understanding the requests package
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now, we will begin our journey with scraping HTML code.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=4)** The request package is the standard Python package to make HTTP request.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=10)** An HTTP request is a message sent by a client to a particular host in order to perform an action on a server.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=18)** This request is made by using components of a URL to provide the information needed to access the resource.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=26)** The request package makes performing these HTTP requests extremely easy, with no need to manually add query strings to URLs or form-encode the data pulled.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=37)** Currently, the request package is utilized 30 million times a week to pull HTTP requests.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=44)** We will thoroughly review how to install and import packages over the next few minutes.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/understanding-the-requests-package?u=76281980&t=50)** This procedure for installing and importing packages is fairly standard for most Python packages available for use.
>

#### How to retrieve webpage information
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we have gathered the HTML code and saved it to our variable response, we can gather some basic information about the webpage.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=8)** First, we can gather the webpage URL as a sanity check to make sure we pulled the correct webpage.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=14)** You can check a scraped webpage's URL by typing the following code response.url.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=22)** Remember that response is our variable name, so make sure to use this variable name where you wish to call it.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=28)** If we run this, we can see we have an output of our url, www.[wisdompetmed.com](https://wisdompetmed.com).
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=36)** This means that we successfully pulled the correct webpage.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=40)** Next, we can gather the HTTP status code of the webpage.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=44)** This http status code is a message the website service sends to the browser to indicate whether the request can be fulfilled or not.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=53)** So in this Jupyter notebook cell here, we are going to type in response.status_code and run it.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=64)** Our output should show as 200.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=69)** We are going to take a moment to explain status codes.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=72)** The status code is always three digits long, and it could be read in two parts.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=76)** The first part contains the first number in the status code.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=80)** This tells us what category the message the server has sent us.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=83)** For example, our status code starting with a two means it is a success response.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=90)** The second part contains the second and third number in the status code.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=94)** This provides us a more thorough description of what our message from the server is telling us.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=100)** In our case, the zero zero paired with the category two stands for, okay.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=105)** This means we successfully made our connection with no other distinctions.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=110)** There are five categories of status codes.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=113)** These include 100s, which are informational codes, 200s which are successful codes, 300s which are redirection codes, 400s which are client error codes, and finally 500s which are server error codes.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=130)** Generally, you won't see a 100s code since they are mainly used to communicate directly between systems on the backend.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=137)** Our goal is to usually receive a 200s code since that means we were able to successfully scrape the website.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=144)** 300s codes are also good to receive, but it usually means you may want to check the URL you are using to see if there is a better option, such as the url it is redirecting you to.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=154)** If you receive a 400s or 500s code, this usually means bad news.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=159)** 400s codes mean the user made a mistake when trying to make the request such as going to a forbidden portion of a website or mistyping the URL making a bad request.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=170)** 500s codes mean the server messed up somehow to where it was unable to successfully load and pull the website for the user.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=177)** For example, the server may be exceeding its bandwidth or there may have been a connection that timed out.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=183)** All of this can sound overwhelming and daunting, especially if you receive a 400s or 500s code.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=190)** Thankfully, you can easily Google the status code you received to determine what exactly it is and potential remedies or explanations on how to fix your request.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=199)** You can also check out this resource for a more thorough explanation of each HTTP status code.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=206)** We are going to head back to Jupyter Notebooks to gather our last piece of information from our webpage.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=212)** In this coding cell, go ahead and type response.headers.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=219)** Retrieving the headers gives us a lot of useful technical information about the webpage we pulled in the form of a Python dictionary.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=226)** For example, we can see the server name, the date we accessed the webpage, the content type, and the connection.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-information?u=76281980&t=235)** All three of these methods can be useful for us to gather more information about the webpage we are scraping, but note that they're not required to use every time you scrape a webpage.

#### How to retrieve HTML code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now we get to see how we can pull all the HTML code we saw on the developer tools into our Python code.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=7)** The first way you could do this is by using the method .content.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=12)** In this cell, we're going to type in response.content and we'll click here to run it.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=19)** In your output, you will notice that we were able to successfully pull the HTML code from the website.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=25)** I suggest you scroll to the top of your output so you can see the beginning of the HTML code.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=31)** You may need to do this for other coding cells as well throughout this course.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=37)** Using .content allows us to see the HTML code in bites which is great to have if we want the machine readable form.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=45)** You can tell it is in bites with the output because it starts with this lowercase B.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=52)** Another method to pull the HTML code is using .text.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=56)** This allows us to have the HTML code from the response variable converted into a string and encoded using UTF-8 versus having it in bite form.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=65)** To do this, we will type in response.text and click over here to run it.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=72)** Here, we can see the output is very similar to the .content output shown previously but now we do not have the lowercase B at the beginning of the output.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=82)** Generally, it is best to scrape webpages using .text to make it easier to read.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-html-code?u=76281980&t=86)** If you seem to be missing important HTML code that is not being converted appropriately with .text, then I suggest switching to using .content instead.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[HTML]] (7), [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]] (1)

#### How to retrieve webpage links
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For our last find all statement, we will find all the links on our webpage.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's start by doing this in this Jupyter Notebook coding cell, and we're going to begin by typing links as our variable and equal it to our variable soup and use the find_all method on it.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980&t=16)** And we want to find all of the a tags.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980&t=20)** These a tags will help us gather any URL links in the code.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980&t=25)** Next, we want to create our for loop.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-retrieve-webpage-links?u=76281980&t=27)** So we'll begin by typing in for, add in our iterator, we'll call it link.

#### What are APIs?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=0)** - I recently consulted with an organization where we had to gather exchange rates data for our analysis.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=7)** Unfortunately, it was difficult for us to find this information online, but we were able to use an API instead to gather the data.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=15)** Let's discuss a bit about what APIs are.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=20)** APIs are a type of software interface that allow two or more software programs to communicate with each other.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=26)** APIs are composed of code that describes how information is exchanged between two or more software programs.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=33)** For example, APIs can help exchange data between a company's database and an application on a computer or cell phone.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=40)** Usually an API acts as a vessel to send and receive information.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=44)** The software that creates the API publishes the information gathered.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=48)** The software that wants to access the information from the API calls the information.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=54)** APIs typically have a software interface where users can use the API's functionality.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=60)** Sometimes there is specific code users can type out to call the information.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=64)** Other times there is a website where users can select what information they wish to pull from the API.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=70)** In this course, we will focus on web APIs, which are a subset of APIs.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=74)** A web API is an API that communicates between a web server and a web browser.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=80)** For example, there's a Twitter API that can read and write data between a coding language like Python and the Twitter social media platform.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=88)** Many of the web APIs are called REST APIs.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=90)** Rest APIs follow the REST software architecture style.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=94)** REST stands for representational state transfer and this style describes a uniform software interface between physically separate components, such as a web API communicating between a web server and a web browser.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=107)** The API we will be working with in this course is called Alpha Vantage and it is also a REST API.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=114)** So why do APIs matter in the first place?
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=117)** APIs allow us to gather information from sources that may be difficult or impossible for us to gather from otherwise.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=123)** For example, some websites do not allow people to scrape from them directly, so maybe we could use an API the website provides to gather that information instead.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-are-apis?u=76281980&t=132)** We will explore the Alpha Vantage API in this chapter.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Env Vars:** api (14), rest (4)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is an  (1), stands for (1), is called (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3), such as (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1), python (1)
> **Speakers:** - i (1)

#### How to use an API key
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] API keys are the way we can access information from an API.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=5)** People and companies who create APIs often require their users to have an API key to access the API data in order to minimize overloading of their API server, track if they're properly using the API, and reduce the amount of potential bots used in the API.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=23)** Usually a API keys are a long series of numbers and letters that act like a password to unlock access to an API.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=31)** Not all APIs require API keys, but each API that does require an API key usually needs a unique one for their particular API versus having one universal API key everyone uses.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=45)** API key should be treated like passwords because they should be kept safe and private.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=50)** If you share your API key with others on a platform such as GitHub or LinkedIn you could potentially have your API access taken away due to too many people accessing the API using your API key.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=64)** One way you can keep your API key safe but still be able to share your code publicly is by using a text file to store your API key.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=73)** This way, whenever you reference your API key in your code it can be hidden behind a variable name.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=80)** You can create a text file by using a text editor on your computer such as TextEdit or Notepad.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=86)** Inside your text file you'll type in your API key and nothing else.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=90)** Once you are done typing, you'll save your file to your computer with your name of choice.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=95)** Here I have an example API key and a text file called API_key_example.txt.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=103)** Please note that this API key is not an actual API key, so make sure you obtain your own personal API key to use the Alpha Vantage API.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=111)** Once your API key is saved into a text file we can bring it into Python.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=117)** I recommend putting your API key text file in the same folder as your Jupyter Notebook file in your code space or on your computer, depending on how you are accessing the code.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-an-api-key?u=76281980&t=127)** This will make it easier to reference since otherwise you'll need to specify the document path in your code.
>

>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-alpha-vantage-functions-part-1?u=76281980&t=228)** If we run this, our output looks very similar to how we had our stock outputs minus having the volume portion, since you'll notice we just have the open, high, low and close.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-use-alpha-vantage-functions-part-1?u=76281980&t=239)** This way we can see on a daily basis what these values are between the US dollar and the Japanese yen.

#### What is text analysis?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the previous chapter, we got a glimpse of some text analysis techniques with using the new sentiment Alpha Vantage function.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=9)** In this chapter, we will explore some techniques for text analysis along with what they can do.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=16)** Text analysis is where we use computers to analyze text data to better understand what is being said and the meaning behind it.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=24)** This is part of what we call natural language processing.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=28)** Natural language processing, also known as NLP, is a subfield of artificial intelligence where computers process and understand human language.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=36)** The input may be written or spoken words for the computer to process.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=41)** We have a wide range of what we can analyze with text analysis.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=45)** For example, we can see what words are popular and show up frequently in our text.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=49)** We can see what the overall sentiment is of the text such as if it is generally positive or negative.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=55)** We can also discover what some common topics are in our text.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=59)** Our analysis in Python will heavily use the NLTK Python package.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=64)** The NLTK package stands for Natural Language Toolkit, and it is an industry standard to perform natural language processing.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=72)** It has over 50 corporate and lexical resources along with libraries for tokenization, stemming, parsing, tagging, classification, and semantic reasoning.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=85)** Why would we want to analyze text data in the first place?
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=88)** Text data is widely used throughout the internet and our daily lives.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=91)** The more we can leverage these analyses, the more we can better understand human behavior, provide language-based solutions, and learn more about text products such as novels and blogs.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=103)** Some applications of natural language processing are translators between two languages, a spell checker on a text application, a virtual assistant device on your computer, and a filter for filtering out spam emails.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=117)** We will explore how to better understand the text from a novel in this chapter.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/what-is-text-analysis?u=76281980&t=121)** Know that the techniques we teach can be expanded upon and use with other text data, such as analyzing product reviews, blog posts, news articles and social media posts.

#### How to pull text data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's head over to Jupyter Notebooks to begin our process of bringing our great_expectations.txt file into our code.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=7)** This text file will be provided to you in Codespaces, but if you are using a different application or program, I highly suggest having this text file in the same folder as your Jupyter Notebook, since otherwise, you will need to specify the path.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=20)** We are going to begin bringing in our text file by creating a variable called textfile and we're going to equal it to open, to open up our file, and then our file is called great_expectations.txt.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=39)** Next we're going to add a comma and put an "r" because we want to read the file.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=44)** And then finally, you want to add an encoding equal to UTF-8.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=49)** You don't always have to add in the encoding UTF-8 but it is good to do just in case it has difficulties reading the text file.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=59)** On the next line, we will have a new variable we will create called great_expect, short for "Great Expectations", and we will equal it to our text file variable, and do a .read on it to read the information from that file.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=75)** Finally, to make sure we bring in the text file correctly, we will print out our variable, great_expect.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-pull-text-data?u=76281980&t=83)** If we run this, we can see the text successfully was pulled into Python with a short introduction about Project Gutenberg.

#### How to create a word cloud

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] We will begin our text analysis by creating a word cloud.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=4)** Word clouds are one way to get a visual representation to see what words are popular in a set of text data.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=10)** Usually word clouds show individual words versus sets of words such as bigrams or sentences.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=16)** Here we can see an example of a word cloud.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=19)** The way we can tell which words are more popular is by their size.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=23)** If the word is larger, then it occurs more frequently in the text.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-create-a-word-cloud?u=76281980&t=27)** Word clouds are often colored, but color does not always indicate how frequently words occur in the text and is often used just to make the word cloud look pretty.

#### How to improve a word cloud
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-improve-a-word-cloud?u=76281980)

#### How to perform Vader sentiment analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now we will learn about the sentiment of our text.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=4)** We will perform a sentiment analysis, which means that we will understand the emotions and whether they're positive, negative, or neutral within our novel.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=12)** We will use the Vader sentiment analysis algorithm to perform our analysis.

> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=154)** Now, we can see for each sentence what our compound, negative, neutral, and positive sentiment scores are.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=160)** A compound score of -1 means the text is 100% negative, a score of 1 means it is 100% positive, and a score of 0 means it is 100% neutral.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=173)** Any values in between means the text is somewhat positive or negative.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=179)** The negative, neutral, and positive columns range from 0 to 1, with 0 meaning it is 100% not that sentiment and 1 meaning that it is 100% that sentiment.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-vader-sentiment-analysis?u=76281980&t=189)** And again, any value in between means that it is somewhat that sentiment.
>

#### How to perform topic modeling
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-topic-modeling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-topic-modeling?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that our dictionary and corpus are created, we can begin building our topic model.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-topic-modeling?u=76281980&t=6)** We will use the Latent Dirichlet Allocation topic model, also known as LDA.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-topic-modeling?u=76281980&t=13)** This algorithm will help us figure out what topics are common in our novel.

> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/how-to-perform-topic-modeling?u=76281980&t=17)** In order to do this analysis, we need to determine what the optimal number of topics is.

> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=174)** Once we've done this, we can now view what our positive, negative, and neutral sentence counts are.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=180)** So this one over here, we want to look at the positive sentences.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=185)** So we are going to look at the compound score, and we want it to be greater than 0.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=191)** And here we also want the compound score, we want it to be less than 0 to get the negative sentences.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=200)** And again, one more compound score here.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=204)** And finally, we can have it be equal to 0 exactly.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=208)** Know that there are multiple ways you can do this.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=210)** You can use the positive, negative, or neutral columns to analyze this.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=215)** But again, in this case, we'll keep it simple and just use that compound column.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=219)** So you'll run this, and we'll see that we have 5,382 total sentences.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=225)** 2,495 of them are positive.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=229)** 1,325 of them are negative.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=232)** And 1,562 of them are neutral.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=236)** So generally, this set of text in our novel is actually more in a positive direction with the sentiment.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/build-three-real-world-python-applications/solution-analyze-sense-and-sensibility?u=76281980&t=244)** Once we are done with this, we will just plot one more thing with our histograms.

>
